<p>The first known king of the third kingdom is <a href="/wiki/Ibbit-Lim" title="Ibbit-Lim">Ibbit-Lim</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMatthiae2013b&#91;httpsbooksgooglecombooksid0H0EpSm8aioCpgPA103_103&#93;_114-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMatthiae2013b[httpsbooksgooglecombooksid0H0EpSm8aioCpgPA103_103]-114"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>102<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> who described himself as the Mekim of Ebla.<sup id="cite_ref-116" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-116"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>note 13<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEArchi2002&#91;httpsbooksgooglecombooksid5a8-NudlBx8CpgPA25_25&#93;_95-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEArchi2002[httpsbooksgooglecombooksid5a8-NudlBx8CpgPA25_25]-95"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>86<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> A basalt votive statue bearing Ibbit-Lim's inscription was discovered in 1968; this helped to identify the site of Tell-Mardikh with the ancient kingdom Ebla.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEArchi2002&#91;httpsbooksgooglecombooksid5a8-NudlBx8CpgPA25_25&#93;_95-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEArchi2002[httpsbooksgooglecombooksid5a8-NudlBx8CpgPA25_25]-95"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>86<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMatthiae2013b&#91;httpsbooksgooglecombooksid0H0EpSm8aioCpgPA103_103&#93;_114-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMatthiae2013b[httpsbooksgooglecombooksid0H0EpSm8aioCpgPA103_103]-114"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>102<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The name of the king is Amorite in the view of Pettinato; it is therefore probable the inhabitants of third kingdom Ebla were predominantly Amorites, as were most of the inhabitants of Syria at that time.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPettinato1991&#91;httpsbooksgooglecombooksidK4JtAAAAMAAJ_22&#93;_117-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPettinato1991[httpsbooksgooglecombooksidK4JtAAAAMAAJ_22]-117"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>104<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>By the beginning of the <span class="nowrap">18th century <span class="smallcaps"><span style="font-variant: small-caps; text-transform: lowercase;">BC</span></span>,</span> Ebla had become a vassal of <a href="/wiki/Yamhad" title="Yamhad">Yamhad</a>, an Amorite kingdom centered in Aleppo.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThuesen2000&#91;httpsbooksgooglecombooksid8qvY8pxVxcwCpgPA61_61&#93;_118-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThuesen2000[httpsbooksgooglecombooksid8qvY8pxVxcwCpgPA61_61]-118"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>105<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFeldman2007&#91;httpsbooksgooglecombooksid_7kP_hNKHxICpgPA55_55&#93;_119-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFeldman2007[httpsbooksgooglecombooksid_7kP_hNKHxICpgPA55_55]-119"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>106<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Written records are not available for this period, but the city was still a vassal during <a href="/wiki/Yarim-Lim_III" title="Yarim-Lim III">Yarim-Lim III</a> of Yamhad's reign.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBryce2014&#91;httpsbooksgooglecombooksidq8Z7AgAAQBAJpgPA25_25&#93;_112-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBryce2014[httpsbooksgooglecombooksidq8Z7AgAAQBAJpgPA25_25]-112"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>100<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> One of the known rulers of Ebla during this period was <a href="/wiki/Immeya" title="Immeya">Immeya</a>, who received gifts from the Egyptian Pharaoh <a href="/wiki/Hotepibre" title="Hotepibre">Hotepibre</a>, indicating the continuing wide connections and importance of Ebla.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMatthiae2008&#91;httpsarchiveorgdetailsbub_gb_gr5BgOwEJicCpagen61_35&#93;_120-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMatthiae2008[httpsarchiveorgdetailsbub_gb_gr5BgOwEJicCpagen61_35]-120"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>107<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The city was mentioned in tablets from the Yamhadite vassal city of <a href="/wiki/Alalakh" title="Alalakh">Alalakh</a> in modern-day Turkey; an Eblaite princess married a son of King <a href="/w/index.php?title=Ammitaqum&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Ammitaqum (page does not exist)">Ammitaqum</a> of Alalakh, who belonged to a branch of the royal <a href="/wiki/Yamhad_dynasty" title="Yamhad dynasty">Yamhadite dynasty</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFrayne1990&#91;httpsbooksgooglecombooksidu2nUT_RtyQ8CpgPA807_807&#93;_121-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFrayne1990[httpsbooksgooglecombooksidu2nUT_RtyQ8CpgPA807_807]-121"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>108<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETeissier1984&#91;httpsbooksgooglecombooksidqu7WeaZA3VUCpgPA72_72&#93;_122-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETeissier1984[httpsbooksgooglecombooksidqu7WeaZA3VUCpgPA72_72]-122"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>109<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Ebla was destroyed by the Hittite King <span class="nowrap"><a href="/wiki/Mursili_I" title="Mursili I">Mursili I</a></span> in about 1600&#160;<span class="smallcaps"><span style="font-variant: small-caps; text-transform: lowercase;">BC</span></span>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBryce2009&#91;httpsbooksgooglecombooksidE1aF0hq1GR8CpgPA211_211&#93;_123-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBryce2009[httpsbooksgooglecombooksidE1aF0hq1GR8CpgPA211_211]-123"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>110<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Indilimma" title="Indilimma">Indilimma</a> was probably the last king of Ebla;<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMatthiae2013b&#91;httpsbooksgooglecombooksid0H0EpSm8aioCpgPA106_106&#93;_124-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMatthiae2013b[httpsbooksgooglecombooksid0H0EpSm8aioCpgPA106_106]-124"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>111<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> a seal of his crown prince Maratewari was discovered in the western palace "Q".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMatthiae2013b&#91;httpsbooksgooglecombooksid0H0EpSm8aioCpgPA106_106&#93;_124-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMatthiae2013b[httpsbooksgooglecombooksid0H0EpSm8aioCpgPA106_106]-124"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>111<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMatthiae2006&#91;httpsbooksgooglecombooksidoYearm8YobQCpgPA86_86&#93;_125-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMatthiae2006[httpsbooksgooglecombooksidoYearm8YobQCpgPA86_86]-125"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>112<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Alternatively, Maratewari  could well be the last king according to Archi,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEArchi2015b24_126-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEArchi2015b24-126"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>113<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> who also argued that the "Song of Release" epic describes the destruction of the third kingdom and preserves older elements.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEArchi2002&#91;httpsbooksgooglecombooksid5a8-NudlBx8CpgPA24_24&#93;_104-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEArchi2002[httpsbooksgooglecombooksid5a8-NudlBx8CpgPA24_24]-104"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>93<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Later_periods">Later periods</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ebla&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Later periods"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>Ebla never recovered from its third destruction. It was a small village in the phase designated "Mardikh&#160;IV" (1600–1200&#160;<span class="smallcaps"><span style="font-variant: small-caps; text-transform: lowercase;">BC</span></span>),<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBryce2009&#91;httpsbooksgooglecombooksidE1aF0hq1GR8CpgPA211_211&#93;_123-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBryce2009[httpsbooksgooglecombooksidE1aF0hq1GR8CpgPA211_211]-123"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>110<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> and was mentioned in the records of Alalakh as a vassal to the <a href="/wiki/Yamhad_dynasty#In_Alalakh" title="Yamhad dynasty">Idrimi dynasty</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAstour1969&#91;httpsbooksgooglecombooksidCMR5C12GOF8CpgPA388_388&#93;_127-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAstour1969[httpsbooksgooglecombooksidCMR5C12GOF8CpgPA388_388]-127"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>114<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> "Mardikh&#160;V" (1200–535&#160;<span class="smallcaps"><span style="font-variant: small-caps; text-transform: lowercase;">BC</span></span>) was a rural, Early Iron Age settlement that grew in size during later periods.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBryce2009&#91;httpsbooksgooglecombooksidE1aF0hq1GR8CpgPA211_211&#93;_123-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBryce2009[httpsbooksgooglecombooksidE1aF0hq1GR8CpgPA211_211]-123"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>110<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Further development occurred during "Mardikh&#160;VI", which lasted until <abbr title="circa">c.</abbr><span style="white-space:nowrap;">&#8201;60</span>&#160;<span class="smallcaps"><span style="font-variant: small-caps; text-transform: lowercase;">AD</span></span>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBryce2009&#91;httpsbooksgooglecombooksidE1aF0hq1GR8CpgPA211_211&#93;_123-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBryce2009[httpsbooksgooglecombooksidE1aF0hq1GR8CpgPA211_211]-123"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>110<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> "Mardikh&#160;VII" began in the <span class="nowrap">3rd century <span class="smallcaps"><span style="font-variant: small-caps; text-transform: lowercase;">AD</span></span></span> and lasted until the 7th&#160;century,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPettinato1991&#91;httpsbooksgooglecombooksidK4JtAAAAMAAJ_16&#93;_128-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPettinato1991[httpsbooksgooglecombooksidK4JtAAAAMAAJ_16]-128"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>115<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> after which the site was abandoned.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHooks1990&#91;httpsbooksgooglecombooksidgoq0VWw9rGICpgPA225_225&#93;_129-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHooks1990[httpsbooksgooglecombooksidgoq0VWw9rGICpgPA225_225]-129"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>116<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Site">Site</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ebla&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Site"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="City_layout">City layout</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ebla&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: City layout"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Ebla_main_sites.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a9/Ebla_main_sites.jpg/220px-Ebla_main_sites.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="303" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a9/Ebla_main_sites.jpg/330px-Ebla_main_sites.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a9/Ebla_main_sites.jpg/440px-Ebla_main_sites.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2016" data-file-height="2778" /></a><figcaption>Ebla's landmarks</figcaption></figure>
<p>Ebla consisted of a lower town and a raised acropolis in the center.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECooper2010&#91;httpsbooksgooglecombooksid46SmAwAAQBAJpgPT130_86&#93;_130-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECooper2010[httpsbooksgooglecombooksid46SmAwAAQBAJpgPT130_86]-130"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>117<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> During the first kingdom, the city had an area of 56 hectares and was protected by mud-brick fortifications.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPinnock2007&#91;httpsbooksgooglecombooksid4926p1H7wV4CpgPA110_110&#93;_131-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPinnock2007[httpsbooksgooglecombooksid4926p1H7wV4CpgPA110_110]-131"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>118<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Ebla was divided into four districts – each with its own gate in the outer wall.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPinnock2007&#91;httpsbooksgooglecombooksid4926p1H7wV4CpgPA112_112&#93;_132-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPinnock2007[httpsbooksgooglecombooksid4926p1H7wV4CpgPA112_112]-132"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>119<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The acropolis included the king's palace&#160;"G",<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGilchrist1995&#91;httpsbooksgooglecombooksidyklDk6Vv0l4CpgPA537_537&#93;_133-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGilchrist1995[httpsbooksgooglecombooksidyklDk6Vv0l4CpgPA537_537]-133"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>120<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> and one of two temples in city dedicated to <a href="/wiki/Kura_(deity)" title="Kura (deity)">Kura</a> (called the "Red Temple").<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPinnock2013&#91;httpsbooksgooglecombooksidqSOYAAAAQBAJpgPA543_543&#93;_134-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPinnock2013[httpsbooksgooglecombooksidqSOYAAAAQBAJpgPA543_543]-134"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>121<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The lower city included the second temple of Kura in the southeast called "Temple of the Rock".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDolce2010&#91;httpsbooksgooglecombooksid9bbgJzacoJYCpgPA251_251&#93;_135-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDolce2010[httpsbooksgooglecombooksid9bbgJzacoJYCpgPA251_251]-135"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>122<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> During the second kingdom, a royal palace (Archaic palace&#160;"P5") was built in the lower town northwest of the acropolis,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEArchi2002&#91;httpsbooksgooglecombooksid5a8-NudlBx8CpgPA26_26&#93;_94-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEArchi2002[httpsbooksgooglecombooksid5a8-NudlBx8CpgPA26_26]-94"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>85<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> in addition to temple "D" built over the destroyed "Red Temple".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMatthiae2013a&#91;httpsbooksgooglecombooksidAaZg0ypYrnQCpgPA40_40&#93;_136-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMatthiae2013a[httpsbooksgooglecombooksidAaZg0ypYrnQCpgPA40_40]-136"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>123<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Ebla_ziggurat.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f9/Ebla_ziggurat.jpg/220px-Ebla_ziggurat.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f9/Ebla_ziggurat.jpg/330px-Ebla_ziggurat.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f9/Ebla_ziggurat.jpg/440px-Ebla_ziggurat.jpg 2x" data-file-width="500" data-file-height="375" /></a><figcaption>Part of a <a href="/wiki/Ziggurat" title="Ziggurat">ziggurat</a> in Ebla</figcaption></figure>
<p>During the third kingdom, Ebla was a large city nearly 60&#160;hectares in size,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMatthiae2008&#91;httpsarchiveorgdetailsbub_gb_gr5BgOwEJicCpagen60_34&#93;_137-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMatthiae2008[httpsarchiveorgdetailsbub_gb_gr5BgOwEJicCpagen60_34]-137"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>124<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> and was protected by a fortified rampart, with double chambered gates.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBonacossi2013&#91;httpsbooksgooglecombooksid5H4fAgAAQBAJpgPT445_421&#93;_138-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBonacossi2013[httpsbooksgooglecombooksid5H4fAgAAQBAJpgPT445_421]-138"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>125<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The acropolis was fortified and separated from the lower town.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBaffiPeyronel2013&#91;httpsbooksgooglecombooksidAaZg0ypYrnQCpgPA200_200&#93;_139-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBaffiPeyronel2013[httpsbooksgooglecombooksidAaZg0ypYrnQCpgPA200_200]-139"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>126<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> New royal palace&#160;"E" was built on the acropolis (during Mardikh IIIB),<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHooks1990&#91;httpsbooksgooglecombooksidgoq0VWw9rGICpgPA226_226&#93;_113-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHooks1990[httpsbooksgooglecombooksidgoq0VWw9rGICpgPA226_226]-113"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>101<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> and a temple of <a href="/wiki/Ishtar" class="mw-redirect" title="Ishtar">Ishtar</a> was constructed over the former "Red" and "D" temples (in area&#160;"D").<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAkkermansSchwartz2003&#91;httpsbooksgooglecombooksid_4oqvpAHDEoCpgPA295_295&#93;_140-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAkkermansSchwartz2003[httpsbooksgooglecombooksid_4oqvpAHDEoCpgPA295_295]-140"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>127<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPinnock2013&#91;httpsbooksgooglecombooksidqSOYAAAAQBAJpgPA543_543&#93;_134-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPinnock2013[httpsbooksgooglecombooksidqSOYAAAAQBAJpgPA543_543]-134"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>121<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The lower town was also divided into four districts;<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPinnock2007&#91;httpsbooksgooglecombooksid4926p1H7wV4CpgPA112_112&#93;_132-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPinnock2007[httpsbooksgooglecombooksid4926p1H7wV4CpgPA112_112]-132"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>119<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> palace&#160;"P5" was used during Mardikh&#160;IIIA,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAstour2002&#91;httpsbooksgooglecombooksid0Rwals-oh6kCpgPA164_164&#93;_141-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAstour2002[httpsbooksgooglecombooksid0Rwals-oh6kCpgPA164_164]-141"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>128<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> and replaced during Mardikh&#160;IIIB by the "Intermediate Palace".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBonacossi2013&#91;httpsbooksgooglecombooksid5H4fAgAAQBAJpgPT445_421&#93;_138-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBonacossi2013[httpsbooksgooglecombooksid5H4fAgAAQBAJpgPT445_421]-138"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>125<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Other third kingdom buildings included the vizier palace,<sup id="cite_ref-143" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-143"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>note 14<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBonacossi2013&#91;httpsbooksgooglecombooksid5H4fAgAAQBAJpgPT446_422&#93;_142-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBonacossi2013[httpsbooksgooglecombooksid5H4fAgAAQBAJpgPT446_422]-142"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>129<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> the western palace (in area&#160;"Q"),<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMatthiae2006&#91;httpsbooksgooglecombooksidoYearm8YobQCpgPA86_86&#93;_125-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMatthiae2006[httpsbooksgooglecombooksidoYearm8YobQCpgPA86_86]-125"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>112<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> the temple of <a href="/wiki/Shamash" title="Shamash">Shamash</a> (temple&#160;"N"), the temple of <a href="/wiki/Resheph" title="Resheph">Rasap</a> (temple&#160;"B1") and the northern palace (built over the "Intermediate Palace").<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBonacossi2013&#91;httpsbooksgooglecombooksid5H4fAgAAQBAJpgPT445_421&#93;_138-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBonacossi2013[httpsbooksgooglecombooksid5H4fAgAAQBAJpgPT445_421]-138"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>125<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPeyronel2008&#91;httpsbooksgooglecombooksidFgML1EdUiWUCpgPA177_177&#93;_144-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPeyronel2008[httpsbooksgooglecombooksidFgML1EdUiWUCpgPA177_177]-144"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>130<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> In the north of the lower town, a second temple for Ishtar was built,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENigro2007&#91;httpsbooksgooglecombooksid4926p1H7wV4CpgPA130_130&#93;_145-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENigro2007[httpsbooksgooglecombooksid4926p1H7wV4CpgPA130_130]-145"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>131<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> while the former "Temple of the Rock" was replaced by a temple of <a href="/wiki/Hadad" title="Hadad">Hadad</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-146" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-146"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>note 15<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENigro2007&#91;httpsbooksgooglecombooksid4926p1H7wV4CpgPA130_130&#93;_145-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENigro2007[httpsbooksgooglecombooksid4926p1H7wV4CpgPA130_130]-145"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>131<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Royal_burials">Royal burials</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ebla&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Royal burials"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<figure class="mw-default-size mw-halign-left" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Ebla.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/36/Ebla.jpg/220px-Ebla.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/36/Ebla.jpg/330px-Ebla.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/36/Ebla.jpg/440px-Ebla.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2272" data-file-height="1704" /></a><figcaption>The western palace&#160;"Q" situated above the royal necropolis</figcaption></figure>
<p>The kings of the first kingdom were buried outside the city; the last ten kings (ending with Irkab-Damu) were buried in <a href="/wiki/Atarib" title="Atarib">Darib</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMichalowski2003&#91;httpsbooksgooglecombooksid8l9X_3rHFdECpgPA465_465&#93;_147-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMichalowski2003[httpsbooksgooglecombooksid8l9X_3rHFdECpgPA465_465]-147"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>132<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> while older kings were buried in a royal mausoleum located in <a href="/wiki/Binnish" title="Binnish">Binas</a> and only one royal tomb dating to the first kingdom was discovered in Ebla (Hypogeum&#160;"G4").<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMatthiae2013&#91;httpsbooksgooglecombooksidO81mDAAAQBAJpgPP57_50&#93;_148-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMatthiae2013[httpsbooksgooglecombooksidO81mDAAAQBAJpgPP57_50]-148"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>133<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> This first kingdom tomb was probably built during the reign of the last king and might be an indication of Eblaite adoption of Mesopotamian traditions to bury the kings beneath their royal palaces.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMatthiae2013&#91;httpsbooksgooglecombooksidO81mDAAAQBAJpgPP57_50&#93;_148-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMatthiae2013[httpsbooksgooglecombooksidO81mDAAAQBAJpgPP57_50]-148"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>133<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>The third kingdom royal necropolis was discovered beneath palace&#160;"Q" (the western palace); it contains many hypogea but only three were excavated.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMatthiae2008&#91;httpsarchiveorgdetailsbub_gb_gr5BgOwEJicCpagen63_37&#93;_149-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMatthiae2008[httpsarchiveorgdetailsbub_gb_gr5BgOwEJicCpagen63_37]-149"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>134<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Those tombs were natural caves in the bedrock of the palace's foundation; they all date to the 19th and <span class="nowrap">18th centuries <span class="smallcaps"><span style="font-variant: small-caps; text-transform: lowercase;">BC</span></span></span> and had a similar plan consisting of an entrance shaft, burial chambers and a dromos connecting the shaft to the chamber.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDolce2010&#91;httpsbooksgooglecombooksid9bbgJzacoJYCpgPA252_252&#93;_88-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDolce2010[httpsbooksgooglecombooksid9bbgJzacoJYCpgPA252_252]-88"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>80<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMogliazzaPolcaro2010&#91;httpsbooksgooglecombooksidVzs8bFFnVeoCpgPA431_431&#93;_150-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMogliazzaPolcaro2010[httpsbooksgooglecombooksidVzs8bFFnVeoCpgPA431_431]-150"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>135<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESuriano2010&#91;httpsbooksgooglecombooksidMfSfzOqEK4sCpgPA60_60&#93;_151-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESuriano2010[httpsbooksgooglecombooksidMfSfzOqEK4sCpgPA60_60]-151"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>136<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading4"><h4 id="Hypogeum_G4">Hypogeum G4</h4><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ebla&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Hypogeum G4"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>The royal tomb found in the royal palace&#160;"G" is designated hypogeum&#160;"G4"; it dates to the archive period, most probably the reign of <a href="/wiki/Isar-Damu" title="Isar-Damu">Isar-Damu</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMatthiae2013&#91;httpsbooksgooglecombooksidAaZg0ypYrnQCpgPA53_53&#93;_152-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMatthiae2013[httpsbooksgooglecombooksidAaZg0ypYrnQCpgPA53_53]-152"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>137<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The tomb is heavily damaged; most of its stones were sacked and nothing of the roof system remains.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELaneri2016&#91;httpsbooksgooglecombooksid2ZehDAAAQBAJpgPA56_56&#93;_153-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELaneri2016[httpsbooksgooglecombooksid2ZehDAAAQBAJpgPA56_56]-153"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>138<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> It also lacks any skeletal remains or funerary goods suggesting that it was either heavily pillaged, never used, or was built as a cenotaph.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELaneri2016&#91;httpsbooksgooglecombooksid2ZehDAAAQBAJpgPA56_56&#93;_153-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELaneri2016[httpsbooksgooglecombooksid2ZehDAAAQBAJpgPA56_56]-153"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>138<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Excavated between 1992 and 1995, it is located underneath the western sector of the palace at a depth of almost 6&#160;meters.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELaneri2016&#91;httpsbooksgooglecombooksid2ZehDAAAQBAJpgPA56_56&#93;_153-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELaneri2016[httpsbooksgooglecombooksid2ZehDAAAQBAJpgPA56_56]-153"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>138<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The tomb is composed of two rooms opened on each other's with lime plaster floors.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELaneri2016&#91;httpsbooksgooglecombooksid2ZehDAAAQBAJpgPA56_56&#93;_153-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELaneri2016[httpsbooksgooglecombooksid2ZehDAAAQBAJpgPA56_56]-153"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>138<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Both rooms are rectangular in shape; the eastern room (L.6402) is 4&#160;meters wide, more than 3,5&#160;meters long (total length is unknown due to heavy damage) and west–east oriented.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMatthiae1997&#91;httpsbooksgooglecombooksidu4F1AAAAMAAJ_270&#93;_154-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMatthiae1997[httpsbooksgooglecombooksidu4F1AAAAMAAJ_270]-154"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>139<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The western room (L.5762) is 5.20 meters long, 4 meters wide and west–east oriented.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMatthiae1997&#91;httpsbooksgooglecombooksidu4F1AAAAMAAJ_269&#93;_155-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMatthiae1997[httpsbooksgooglecombooksidu4F1AAAAMAAJ_269]-155"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>140<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Limestone was used to build the walls and few blocks protruding from the sides toward the middle of the rooms suggest the roof to have been a <a href="/wiki/Corbel_arch" title="Corbel arch">corbelled vault</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELaneri2016&#91;httpsbooksgooglecombooksid2ZehDAAAQBAJpgPA56_56&#93;_153-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELaneri2016[httpsbooksgooglecombooksid2ZehDAAAQBAJpgPA56_56]-153"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>138<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMatthiae1997&#91;httpsbooksgooglecombooksidu4F1AAAAMAAJ_270&#93;_154-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMatthiae1997[httpsbooksgooglecombooksidu4F1AAAAMAAJ_270]-154"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>139<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading4"><h4 id="Western_palace_tombs">Western palace tombs</h4><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ebla&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Western palace tombs"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Eblaite_tombs.png" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ac/Eblaite_tombs.png/220px-Eblaite_tombs.png" decoding="async" width="220" height="195" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ac/Eblaite_tombs.png/330px-Eblaite_tombs.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ac/Eblaite_tombs.png/440px-Eblaite_tombs.png 2x" data-file-width="1688" data-file-height="1496" /></a><figcaption>Plan of the western palace tombs</figcaption></figure>
<p>The tombs were found under the floor of Building Q, which was built in the Isin-Larsa period.<sup id="cite_ref-156" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-156"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>141<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<ul><li><b>The tomb of the princess</b>: dating to <abbr title="circa">c.</abbr><span style="white-space:nowrap;">&#8201;1800</span>&#160;<span class="smallcaps"><span style="font-variant: small-caps; text-transform: lowercase;">BC</span></span>, it is the oldest and smallest of the third kingdom tombs found.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESuriano2010&#91;httpsbooksgooglecombooksidMfSfzOqEK4sCpgPA60_60&#93;_151-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESuriano2010[httpsbooksgooglecombooksidMfSfzOqEK4sCpgPA60_60]-151"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>136<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMatthiae1989&#91;httpsbooksgooglecombooksidiWZtAAAAMAAJ_303&#93;_157-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMatthiae1989[httpsbooksgooglecombooksidiWZtAAAAMAAJ_303]-157"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>142<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Excavated in 1978,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMatthiae1989&#91;httpsbooksgooglecombooksidiWZtAAAAMAAJ_303&#93;_157-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMatthiae1989[httpsbooksgooglecombooksidiWZtAAAAMAAJ_303]-157"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>142<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> it contained the remains of a young woman, hence the naming.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMatthiae198424_158-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMatthiae198424-158"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>143<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The dromos has steps, partially cut in the bedrock and partially paved with stones, leading to the chamber, which was achieved through the enlargement of a natural cave.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENigro2009&#91;httpswwwacademiaedu1096021The_Eighteenth_Century_BC_Princes_of_Byblos_and_Ebla_and_the_Chronology_of_the_Middle_Bronze_Age_161&#93;_159-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENigro2009[httpswwwacademiaedu1096021The_Eighteenth_Century_BC_Princes_of_Byblos_and_Ebla_and_the_Chronology_of_the_Middle_Bronze_Age_161]-159"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>144<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The tomb is the only one not pillaged;<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPettinato1991&#91;httpsbooksgooglecombooksidK4JtAAAAMAAJ_28&#93;_160-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPettinato1991[httpsbooksgooglecombooksidK4JtAAAAMAAJ_28]-160"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>145<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> it contained precious jewels and funerary objects.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGrajetzki2013&#91;httpsbooksgooglecombooksidm0MoAgAAQBAJpgPA173_173&#93;_161-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGrajetzki2013[httpsbooksgooglecombooksidm0MoAgAAQBAJpgPA173_173]-161"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>146<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup></li>
<li><b>The tomb of the cisterns</b>: this tomb is the most damaged in the necropolis.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESuriano2010&#91;httpsbooksgooglecombooksidMfSfzOqEK4sCpgPA60_60&#93;_151-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESuriano2010[httpsbooksgooglecombooksidMfSfzOqEK4sCpgPA60_60]-151"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>136<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> It consists of a double room burial; the earliest, (Q79A), was built at the same period of the tomb of the princess, and was badly damaged when the tomb was reused, and a dromos was built in the place of Q79A toward the end of the <span class="nowrap">17th century <span class="smallcaps"><span style="font-variant: small-caps; text-transform: lowercase;">BC</span></span></span> (leading to the founding of burial Q79B).<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENigro2009&#91;httpswwwacademiaedu1096021The_Eighteenth_Century_BC_Princes_of_Byblos_and_Ebla_and_the_Chronology_of_the_Middle_Bronze_Age_161&#93;_159-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENigro2009[httpswwwacademiaedu1096021The_Eighteenth_Century_BC_Princes_of_Byblos_and_Ebla_and_the_Chronology_of_the_Middle_Bronze_Age_161]-159"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>144<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> This was probably the resting place of a king; a club (a symbol of royal power) was discovered in Q79A.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENigro2009&#91;httpswwwacademiaedu1096021The_Eighteenth_Century_BC_Princes_of_Byblos_and_Ebla_and_the_Chronology_of_the_Middle_Bronze_Age_161&#93;_159-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENigro2009[httpswwwacademiaedu1096021The_Eighteenth_Century_BC_Princes_of_Byblos_and_Ebla_and_the_Chronology_of_the_Middle_Bronze_Age_161]-159"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>144<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup></li>
<li><b>The tomb of the lord of the goats</b>: it is the largest in the necropolis; it includes two depositional chambers and is reached through a vertical shaft.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENigro2009&#91;httpswwwacademiaedu1096021The_Eighteenth_Century_BC_Princes_of_Byblos_and_Ebla_and_the_Chronology_of_the_Middle_Bronze_Age_161&#93;_159-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENigro2009[httpswwwacademiaedu1096021The_Eighteenth_Century_BC_Princes_of_Byblos_and_Ebla_and_the_Chronology_of_the_Middle_Bronze_Age_161]-159"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>144<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The occupier of the tomb is not known with certainty, he is called the lord of the goats by archaeologists due to the existence of a throne decorated with bronze goat heads in the tomb.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFortin1999&#91;httpsbooksgooglecombooksidiFttAAAAMAAJ_173&#93;_162-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFortin1999[httpsbooksgooglecombooksidiFttAAAAMAAJ_173]-162"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>147<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> A silver cup that has the name of king <a href="/wiki/Immeya" title="Immeya">Immeya</a> inscribed was found in the tomb making that king the most likely owner of the burial.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMatthiae2010&#91;httpsbooksgooglecombooksidnoPMQgAACAAJ_217&#93;_163-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMatthiae2010[httpsbooksgooglecombooksidnoPMQgAACAAJ_217]-163"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>148<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup></li></ul>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Government">Government</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ebla&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Government"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1236090951"><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/List_of_kings_of_Ebla" title="List of kings of Ebla">List of kings of Ebla</a></div>
<p>The first kingdom's government consisted of the king (styled <i>Malikum</i>) and the grand vizier, who headed a council of elders (<i>Abbu</i>) and the administration.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFiner1997&#91;httpsbooksgooglecombooksidaEziNfjinnMCpgPA172_172&#93;_164-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFiner1997[httpsbooksgooglecombooksidaEziNfjinnMCpgPA172_172]-164"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>149<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The second kingdom was also a monarchy,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAstour2002&#91;httpsbooksgooglecombooksid0Rwals-oh6kCpgPA132_132&#93;_102-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAstour2002[httpsbooksgooglecombooksid0Rwals-oh6kCpgPA132_132]-102"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>91<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> but little is known about it because of a lack of written records.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAstour2002&#91;httpsbooksgooglecombooksid0Rwals-oh6kCpgPA76_76&#93;_89-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAstour2002[httpsbooksgooglecombooksid0Rwals-oh6kCpgPA76_76]-89"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>81<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The third kingdom was a city-state monarchy with reduced importance under the authority of Yamhad.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHamblin2006&#91;httpsbooksgooglecombooksidbiyDDd0uKGMCpgPT261_267&#93;_165-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHamblin2006[httpsbooksgooglecombooksidbiyDDd0uKGMCpgPT261_267]-165"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>150<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Administration_of_the_first_kingdom">Administration of the first kingdom</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ebla&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Administration of the first kingdom"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1236090951"><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Vizier_(Ebla)" title="Vizier (Ebla)">Vizier (Ebla)</a></div>
<p>The queen shared the running of affairs of state with the king.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGilchrist1995&#91;httpsbooksgooglecombooksidyklDk6Vv0l4CpgPA537_537&#93;_133-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGilchrist1995[httpsbooksgooglecombooksidyklDk6Vv0l4CpgPA537_537]-133"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>120<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The crown prince was involved in internal matters and the second prince was involved in foreign affairs.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGilchrist1995&#91;httpsbooksgooglecombooksidyklDk6Vv0l4CpgPA537_537&#93;_133-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGilchrist1995[httpsbooksgooglecombooksidyklDk6Vv0l4CpgPA537_537]-133"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>120<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Most duties, including military ones, were handled by the vizier and the administration, which consisted of 13&#160;court dignitaries – each of whom controlled between 400 and 800&#160;men forming a bureaucracy with 11,700&#160;people.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFiner1997&#91;httpsbooksgooglecombooksidaEziNfjinnMCpgPA172_172&#93;_164-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFiner1997[httpsbooksgooglecombooksidaEziNfjinnMCpgPA172_172]-164"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>149<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Each of the four quarters of the lower city was governed by a chief inspector and many deputies.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGilchrist1995&#91;httpsbooksgooglecombooksidyklDk6Vv0l4CpgPA537_537&#93;_133-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGilchrist1995[httpsbooksgooglecombooksidyklDk6Vv0l4CpgPA537_537]-133"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>120<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> To oversee royal interest, the king employed agents (mashkim), collectors (<i>ur</i>) and messengers (<i>kas</i>).<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHamblin2006&#91;httpsbooksgooglecombooksidbiyDDd0uKGMCpgPT240_242&#93;_39-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHamblin2006[httpsbooksgooglecombooksidbiyDDd0uKGMCpgPT240_242]-39"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>36<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading4"><h4 id="Administrative_divisions">Administrative divisions</h4><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ebla&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Administrative divisions"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>Many client kingdoms owed allegiance to Ebla and each was ruled by its own king (En); those vassal kings were highly autonomous, paying tribute and supplying military assistance to Ebla.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHamblin2006&#91;httpsbooksgooglecombooksidbiyDDd0uKGMCpgPT240_242&#93;_39-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHamblin2006[httpsbooksgooglecombooksidbiyDDd0uKGMCpgPT240_242]-39"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>36<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The administrative center in the capital was named the "SA.ZA"; it included the royal palaces, storerooms and some temples.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBiga2013&#91;httpsbooksgooglecombooksidAaZg0ypYrnQCpgPA265_265&#93;_166-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBiga2013[httpsbooksgooglecombooksidAaZg0ypYrnQCpgPA265_265]-166"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>151<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Regions beyond the walls of the capital were collectively named in Eblaite texts "uru-bar" (literally meaning outside of the city).<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBiga2013&#91;httpsbooksgooglecombooksidAaZg0ypYrnQCpgPA265_265&#93;_166-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBiga2013[httpsbooksgooglecombooksidAaZg0ypYrnQCpgPA265_265]-166"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>151<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The villages and towns under the central authority were either ruled directly from the capital,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBiga2013&#91;httpsbooksgooglecombooksidAaZg0ypYrnQCpgPA265_265&#93;_166-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBiga2013[httpsbooksgooglecombooksidAaZg0ypYrnQCpgPA265_265]-166"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>151<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> or had appointed officials.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBiga2013&#91;httpsbooksgooglecombooksidAaZg0ypYrnQCpgPA265_265&#93;_166-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBiga2013[httpsbooksgooglecombooksidAaZg0ypYrnQCpgPA265_265]-166"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>151<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The titles of the civil servants do not clearly define the bearer's responsibilities and authority as each town had its own political traditions.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECooper2006&#91;httpsbooksgooglecombooksidFQ-AAgAAQBAJpgPT91_91&#93;_167-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECooper2006[httpsbooksgooglecombooksidFQ-AAgAAQBAJpgPT91_91]-167"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>152<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<ul><li>Lugal: while in Mesopotamia a <span title="uncoded-language text"><span lang="mis" style="font-style: normal;">lugal</span></span> designated a king, in Ebla it designated a governor who was directly under the authority of the capital.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPongratz-Leisten2015&#91;httpsbooksgooglecombooksidLJqnCgAAQBAJpgPA199_199&#93;_168-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPongratz-Leisten2015[httpsbooksgooglecombooksidLJqnCgAAQBAJpgPA199_199]-168"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>153<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The nature of this title as part of Eblaite bureaucracy is ambiguous; each <span title="uncoded-language text"><span lang="mis" style="font-style: normal;">lugal</span></span> was under the authority of the grand vizier,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELiverani2013&#91;httpsbooksgooglecombooksid0d1JAgAAQBAJpgPA122_122&#93;_169-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELiverani2013[httpsbooksgooglecombooksid0d1JAgAAQBAJpgPA122_122]-169"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>154<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> and the bearers ruled cities directly under the authority of the capital and they all brought goods to be kept in Ebla's storehouses.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBiga2013&#91;httpsbooksgooglecombooksidAaZg0ypYrnQCpgPA265_265&#93;_166-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBiga2013[httpsbooksgooglecombooksidAaZg0ypYrnQCpgPA265_265]-166"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>151<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Pettinato counted 14 different <span title="uncoded-language romanization"><span style="font-style: normal" lang="mis-Latn">lugals</span></span> in the Eblaite administrative texts and deduced that the kingdom was divided into fourteen departments; two of them in the capital itself and the remaining twelve spanned the rest of the kingdom.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStieglitz2002&#91;httpsbooksgooglecombooksid0Rwals-oh6kCpgPA215_215&#93;_170-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStieglitz2002[httpsbooksgooglecombooksid0Rwals-oh6kCpgPA215_215]-170"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>155<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup></li>
<li>Ugula: the title is translated as superintendent; some <span title="uncoded-language romanization"><span style="font-style: normal" lang="mis-Latn">ugulas</span></span> were independent rulers and some represented the highest authority of a tribal group.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBiga2013&#91;httpsbooksgooglecombooksidAaZg0ypYrnQCpgPA260_260&#93;_171-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBiga2013[httpsbooksgooglecombooksidAaZg0ypYrnQCpgPA260_260]-171"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>156<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Many cities had an appointed <span title="uncoded-language romanization"><span style="font-style: normal" lang="mis-Latn">ugula</span></span> as their head of administration such as the city of Darum.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBiga2013&#91;httpsbooksgooglecombooksidAaZg0ypYrnQCpgPA265_265&#93;_166-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBiga2013[httpsbooksgooglecombooksidAaZg0ypYrnQCpgPA265_265]-166"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>151<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup></li></ul>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading4"><h4 id="The_chora">The chora</h4><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ebla&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: The chora"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>The regions under the direct control of the king that were economically vital for the capital are called the "<a href="/wiki/Kh%C3%B4ra" title="Khôra">chora</a>" by archaeologists.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMatthiae2013a&#91;httpsbooksgooglecombooksidAaZg0ypYrnQCpgPA37_37&#93;_12-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMatthiae2013a[httpsbooksgooglecombooksidAaZg0ypYrnQCpgPA37_37]-12"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>11<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBiga2013&#91;httpsbooksgooglecombooksidAaZg0ypYrnQCpgPA266_266&#93;_172-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBiga2013[httpsbooksgooglecombooksidAaZg0ypYrnQCpgPA266_266]-172"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>157<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Regions under direct control of the king extended beyond the chora and it is difficult to determine the exact size of the kingdom and the chora due to the constant military expansion of Ebla which added new territories; some of those were ruled directly while others were allowed to retain their own rulers as vassals.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBiga2013&#91;httpsbooksgooglecombooksidAaZg0ypYrnQCpgPA266_266&#93;_172-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBiga2013[httpsbooksgooglecombooksidAaZg0ypYrnQCpgPA266_266]-172"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>157<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Generally, the chora is the core region of Ebla that includes the economic hinterland supporting the capital.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMatthiae2013a&#91;httpsbooksgooglecombooksidAaZg0ypYrnQCpgPA37_37&#93;_12-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMatthiae2013a[httpsbooksgooglecombooksidAaZg0ypYrnQCpgPA37_37]-12"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>11<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> It includes the cities and villages where the king or his vizier had palaces, towns that included important sanctuaries of gods related to the royal institution, towns visited by the monarch during the different rituals he participated in (such as the renewal of royalty ritual),<sup id="cite_ref-174" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-174"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>note 16<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> and other cities such as the ones where textiles were delivered.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBiga2014&#91;httpsbooksgooglecombooksidAcnmBQAAQBAJpgPA94_94&#93;_175-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBiga2014[httpsbooksgooglecombooksidAcnmBQAAQBAJpgPA94_94]-175"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>159<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The chora spans around 3000&#160;km<sup>2</sup>; from west to east it includes the plains east of <a href="/wiki/Jabal_Zawiya" title="Jabal Zawiya">Jabal Zawiya</a>, the Maṭkh swamp, <a href="/wiki/Mount_al-Hass" title="Mount al-Hass">al-Hass mountain</a> and mount Shabīth.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTED&#39;AndreaAscalone2013&#91;httpsbooksgooglecombooksidAaZg0ypYrnQCpgPA215_215&#93;_176-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTED&#39;AndreaAscalone2013[httpsbooksgooglecombooksidAaZg0ypYrnQCpgPA215_215]-176"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>160<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Areas directly on the borders of the chora such as <a href="/wiki/Al-Ghab_Plain" class="mw-redirect" title="Al-Ghab Plain">al-Ghab</a>, <a href="/wiki/Harim_Mountains" title="Harim Mountains">al-Rouge plain</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sabkhat_al-Jabbul" title="Sabkhat al-Jabbul">al-Jabbul</a> have close cultural affinity with the chora.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTED&#39;AndreaAscalone2013&#91;httpsbooksgooglecombooksidAaZg0ypYrnQCpgPA215_215&#93;_176-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTED&#39;AndreaAscalone2013[httpsbooksgooglecombooksidAaZg0ypYrnQCpgPA215_215]-176"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>160<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="People,_language,_and_culture"><span id="People.2C_language.2C_and_culture"></span>People, language, and culture</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ebla&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: People, language, and culture"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="The_first_and_second_kingdoms">The first and second kingdoms</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ebla&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: The first and second kingdoms"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Ebla6.JPG" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cb/Ebla6.JPG/220px-Ebla6.JPG" decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cb/Ebla6.JPG/330px-Ebla6.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cb/Ebla6.JPG/440px-Ebla6.JPG 2x" data-file-width="1600" data-file-height="1200" /></a><figcaption>Royal palace&#160;"G" courtyard</figcaption></figure>
<p>Mardikh II's periods shared the same culture.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAstour2002&#91;httpsbooksgooglecombooksid0Rwals-oh6kCpgPA164_164&#93;_141-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAstour2002[httpsbooksgooglecombooksid0Rwals-oh6kCpgPA164_164]-141"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>128<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> the population of Ebla during Mardikh&#160;IIB1 (2400–2300 BC) is estimated to have numbered around 40,000 in the capital, and over 200,000&#160;people in the entire kingdom.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKohl1991&#91;httpsbooksgooglecombooksiduc3ZokKnaQYCpgPA230_230&#93;_177-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKohl1991[httpsbooksgooglecombooksiduc3ZokKnaQYCpgPA230_230]-177"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>161<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The Eblaites of Mardikh II were <a href="/wiki/Semitic_languages" title="Semitic languages">Semite-speakers</a> close to their Northwestern Semitic neighbors, such as the Amorites.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERendsburg1997&#91;httpoxfordbiblicalstudiescomarticleoprt256e315_hi4_pos56_183&#93;_178-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERendsburg1997[httpoxfordbiblicalstudiescomarticleoprt256e315_hi4_pos56_183]-178"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>162<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Giovanni Pettinato said the <a href="/wiki/Eblaite_language" title="Eblaite language">Eblaite language</a>, one of the oldest attested Semitic languages,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBernal2006&#91;httpsbooksgooglecombooksidG8Ltdr2rg04CpgPA591_591&#93;_179-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBernal2006[httpsbooksgooglecombooksidG8Ltdr2rg04CpgPA591_591]-179"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>163<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> was a <a href="/wiki/West_Semitic_languages" title="West Semitic languages">West Semitic language</a>; Gelb and others said it was an <a href="/wiki/East_Semitic" class="mw-redirect" title="East Semitic">East Semitic</a> dialect closer to the <a href="/wiki/Akkadian_language" title="Akkadian language">Akkadian language</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFaber2013&#91;httpsbooksgooglecombooksidaWhHAQAAQBAJpgPA7_7&#93;_180-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFaber2013[httpsbooksgooglecombooksidaWhHAQAAQBAJpgPA7_7]-180"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>164<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Academic consensus considers Eblaite an East Semitic language which exhibits both West and East Semitic features.<sup id="cite_ref-181" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-181"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>note 17<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStiebing2016&#91;httpsbooksgooglecombooksidDoyTDAAAQBAJpgPA63_63&#93;_182-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStiebing2016[httpsbooksgooglecombooksidDoyTDAAAQBAJpgPA63_63]-182"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>165<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGordon2013&#91;httpsbooksgooglecombooksidaWhHAQAAQBAJpgPA101_101&#93;_183-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGordon2013[httpsbooksgooglecombooksidaWhHAQAAQBAJpgPA101_101]-183"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>166<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Ebla held several religious and social festivals, including rituals for the succession of a new king, which normally lasted for several weeks.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDolansky2013&#91;httpsbooksgooglecombooksid7anCAQAAQBAJpgPA66_66&#93;_184-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDolansky2013[httpsbooksgooglecombooksid7anCAQAAQBAJpgPA66_66]-184"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>167<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The Eblaite calendars were based on a <a href="/wiki/Tropical_year" title="Tropical year">solar year</a> divided into twelve months.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPettinato1981&#91;httpsarchiveorgdetailsarchivesofeblaan00pett_147&#93;_185-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPettinato1981[httpsarchiveorgdetailsarchivesofeblaan00pett_147]-185"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>168<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Two calendars were discovered; the "old calendar" used during the reign of Igrish-Halam, and a "new calendar" introduced by vizier Ibbi-Sipish.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPettinato1981&#91;httpsarchiveorgdetailsarchivesofeblaan00pett_147&#93;_185-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPettinato1981[httpsarchiveorgdetailsarchivesofeblaan00pett_147]-185"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>168<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Many months were named in honor of deities; in the new calendar, "Itu be-li" was the first month of the year, and meant "the month of the lord".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEShea1981&#91;httpdigitalcommonsandrewseduaussvol19iss114_60&#93;_186-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEShea1981[httpdigitalcommonsandrewseduaussvol19iss114_60]-186"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>169<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Each year was given a name instead of a number.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPodany2010&#91;httpsbooksgooglecombooksid_ez3ih5JgzUCpgPA34_34&#93;_187-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPodany2010[httpsbooksgooglecombooksid_ez3ih5JgzUCpgPA34_34]-187"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>170<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Women received salaries equal to those of men and could accede to important positions and head government agencies.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPettinato1991&#91;httpsbooksgooglecombooksidK4JtAAAAMAAJ_75&#93;_188-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPettinato1991[httpsbooksgooglecombooksidK4JtAAAAMAAJ_75]-188"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>171<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The Eblaites imported <a href="/wiki/Kunga_(equid)" title="Kunga (equid)">Kungas</a> from <a href="/wiki/Tell_Brak#Kingdom_of_Nagar" title="Tell Brak">Nagar</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-190" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-190"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>note 18<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPeyronelVacca2013&#91;httpsbooksgooglecombooksidAaZg0ypYrnQCpgPA436_436&#93;_191-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPeyronelVacca2013[httpsbooksgooglecombooksidAaZg0ypYrnQCpgPA436_436]-191"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>173<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> and used them to draw the carriages of royalty and high officials, as well as diplomatic gifts for allied cities.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPeyronelVacca2013&#91;httpsbooksgooglecombooksidAaZg0ypYrnQCpgPA436_436&#93;_191-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPeyronelVacca2013[httpsbooksgooglecombooksidAaZg0ypYrnQCpgPA436_436]-191"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>173<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Society was less centered around the palace and the temple than in Mesopotamian kingdoms. The Eblaite palace was designed around the courtyard, which was open toward the city, thus making the administration approachable. This contrasts with Mesopotamian palaces, which resembled citadels with narrow entrances and limited access to the external courtyard.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELiverani2013&#91;httpsbooksgooglecombooksid0d1JAgAAQBAJpgPA127_127&#93;_192-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELiverani2013[httpsbooksgooglecombooksid0d1JAgAAQBAJpgPA127_127]-192"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>174<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Music played an important part in the society and musicians were both locals,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETonietti2010&#91;httpsbooksgooglecombooksid-H3iYPUc97YCpgPA73_73&#93;_193-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETonietti2010[httpsbooksgooglecombooksid-H3iYPUc97YCpgPA73_73]-193"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>175<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> or hired from other cities such as Mari.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETonietti2010&#91;httpsbooksgooglecombooksid-H3iYPUc97YCpgPA76_76&#93;_194-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETonietti2010[httpsbooksgooglecombooksid-H3iYPUc97YCpgPA76_76]-194"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>176<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Ebla also hired acrobats from Nagar, but later reduced their number and kept some to train local Eblaite acrobats.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEidemFinkelBonechi2001&#91;httpswwwacademiaedu5261148The_Third-millennium_Inscriptions_101&#93;_195-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEidemFinkelBonechi2001[httpswwwacademiaedu5261148The_Third-millennium_Inscriptions_101]-195"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>177<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="The_third_kingdom">The third kingdom</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ebla&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: The third kingdom"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>The Mardikh III population was predominately Semitic Amorite.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPettinato1991&#91;httpsbooksgooglecombooksidK4JtAAAAMAAJ_22&#93;_117-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPettinato1991[httpsbooksgooglecombooksidK4JtAAAAMAAJ_22]-117"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>104<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The Amorites were mentioned in the first kingdom's tablets as neighbors and as rural subjects,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELönnqvist2008&#91;httpsbooksgooglecombooksidJ9eZToFfVJ0CpgPA205_205&#93;_196-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELönnqvist2008[httpsbooksgooglecombooksidJ9eZToFfVJ0CpgPA205_205]-196"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>178<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> and they came to dominate Ebla after the destruction of the second kingdom.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEArmstrong1996&#91;httpsbooksgooglecombooksidystMAgAAQBAJpgPA191_191&#93;_197-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEArmstrong1996[httpsbooksgooglecombooksidystMAgAAQBAJpgPA191_191]-197"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>179<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The city witnessed a great increase in construction, and many palaces, temples and fortifications were built.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKuhrt1995&#91;httpsbooksgooglecombooksidV_sfMzRPTgoCpgPA75_75&#93;_198-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKuhrt1995[httpsbooksgooglecombooksidV_sfMzRPTgoCpgPA75_75]-198"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>180<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The Amorite-speaking Eblaites worshiped many of the same deities as the Paleo-Syrian-speaking Eblaites of earlier periods,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEArchi2002&#91;httpsbooksgooglecombooksid5a8-NudlBx8CpgPA31_31&#93;_199-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEArchi2002[httpsbooksgooglecombooksid5a8-NudlBx8CpgPA31_31]-199"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>181<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> and maintained the sanctity of the acropolis in the center of the city.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDolce2010&#91;httpsbooksgooglecombooksid9bbgJzacoJYCpgPA252_252&#93;_88-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDolce2010[httpsbooksgooglecombooksid9bbgJzacoJYCpgPA252_252]-88"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>80<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The third kingdom's iconography and royal ideology were under the influence of Yamhad's culture; kingship was received from the Yamhadite deities instead of Ishtar of Ebla, which is evident by the Eblaite seals of Indilimma's period.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMatthiae2003&#91;httpsbooksgooglecombooksidkiWD_5PRQBUCpgPA393_393&#93;_200-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMatthiae2003[httpsbooksgooglecombooksidkiWD_5PRQBUCpgPA393_393]-200"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>182<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Economy">Economy</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ebla&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Economy"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>During the first kingdom period, the palace controlled the economy,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELiverani2013&#91;httpsbooksgooglecombooksid0d1JAgAAQBAJpgPA122_122&#93;_169-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELiverani2013[httpsbooksgooglecombooksid0d1JAgAAQBAJpgPA122_122]-169"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>154<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> but wealthy families managed their financial affairs without government intervention.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPettinato1991&#91;httpsbooksgooglecombooksidK4JtAAAAMAAJ_168&#93;_201-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPettinato1991[httpsbooksgooglecombooksidK4JtAAAAMAAJ_168]-201"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>183<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The economic system was redistributive; the palace distributed food to its permanent and seasonal workers. It is estimated that around 40,000 persons contributed to this system, but in general, and unlike in Mesopotamia, land stayed in the hands of villages, which paid an annual share to the palace.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELiverani2013&#91;httpsbooksgooglecombooksid0d1JAgAAQBAJpgPA124_124&#93;_202-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELiverani2013[httpsbooksgooglecombooksid0d1JAgAAQBAJpgPA124_124]-202"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>184<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Agriculture was mainly pastoral; large herds of cattle were managed by the palace.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELiverani2013&#91;httpsbooksgooglecombooksid0d1JAgAAQBAJpgPA124_124&#93;_202-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELiverani2013[httpsbooksgooglecombooksid0d1JAgAAQBAJpgPA124_124]-202"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>184<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The city's inhabitants owned around 140,000 head of sheep and goats, and 9,000 cattle.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELiverani2013&#91;httpsbooksgooglecombooksid0d1JAgAAQBAJpgPA124_124&#93;_202-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELiverani2013[httpsbooksgooglecombooksid0d1JAgAAQBAJpgPA124_124]-202"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>184<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Ebla derived its prosperity from trade;<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELiverani2013&#91;httpsbooksgooglecombooksid0d1JAgAAQBAJpgPA124_124&#93;_202-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELiverani2013[httpsbooksgooglecombooksid0d1JAgAAQBAJpgPA124_124]-202"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>184<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> its wealth was equal to that of the most important Sumerian cities,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELiverani2013&#91;httpsbooksgooglecombooksid0d1JAgAAQBAJpgPA129_129&#93;_203-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELiverani2013[httpsbooksgooglecombooksid0d1JAgAAQBAJpgPA129_129]-203"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>185<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> and its main commercial rival was Mari.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBryce2014&#91;httpsbooksgooglecombooksidq8Z7AgAAQBAJpgPA16_16&#93;_72-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBryce2014[httpsbooksgooglecombooksidq8Z7AgAAQBAJpgPA16_16]-72"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>65<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Ebla's main articles of trade were probably timber from the nearby mountains, and textiles.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMarchesi2013&#91;httpsbooksgooglecombooksidAaZg0ypYrnQCpgPA274_274&#93;_204-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMarchesi2013[httpsbooksgooglecombooksidAaZg0ypYrnQCpgPA274_274]-204"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>186<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Handicrafts also appear to have been a major export, evidenced by the quantity of artifacts recovered from the palaces of the city.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAkkermansSchwartz2003&#91;httpsbooksgooglecombooksid_4oqvpAHDEoCpgPA271_271&#93;_205-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAkkermansSchwartz2003[httpsbooksgooglecombooksid_4oqvpAHDEoCpgPA271_271]-205"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>187<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Ebla possessed a wide commercial network reaching as far as modern-day Afghanistan.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELiverani2013&#91;httpsbooksgooglecombooksid0d1JAgAAQBAJpgPA126_126&#93;_206-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELiverani2013[httpsbooksgooglecombooksid0d1JAgAAQBAJpgPA126_126]-206"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>188<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> It shipped textiles to Cyprus, possibly through the port of <a href="/wiki/Ugarit" title="Ugarit">Ugarit</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDahood1978&#91;httpsbooksgooglecombooksiduLNDUDGTOz4CpgPA83_83&#93;_207-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDahood1978[httpsbooksgooglecombooksiduLNDUDGTOz4CpgPA83_83]-207"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>189<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> but most of its trade seems to have been directed by river-boat towards Mesopotamia – chiefly Kish.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPettinato1991&#91;httpsbooksgooglecombooksidK4JtAAAAMAAJ_155&#93;_208-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPettinato1991[httpsbooksgooglecombooksidK4JtAAAAMAAJ_155]-208"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>190<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The main palace <i>G</i> was found to contain artifacts dating from <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Egypt" title="Ancient Egypt">Ancient Egypt</a> bearing the names of the pharaohs <a href="/wiki/Khafre" title="Khafre">Khafre</a> and <a href="/wiki/Pepi_I" class="mw-redirect" title="Pepi I">Pepi I</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAruz2003&#91;httpsbooksgooglecombooksid8l9X_3rHFdECpgPA241_241&#93;_209-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAruz2003[httpsbooksgooglecombooksid8l9X_3rHFdECpgPA241_241]-209"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>191<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Ebla continued to be a center of trade during the second kingdom, evidenced by the surrounding cities that appeared during its period and were destroyed along with the city.<sup id="cite_ref-210" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-210"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>note 19<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAstour2002&#91;httpsbooksgooglecombooksid0Rwals-oh6kCpgPA78_78&#93;_83-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAstour2002[httpsbooksgooglecombooksid0Rwals-oh6kCpgPA78_78]-83"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>75<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Trade continued to be Ebla's main economic activity during the third kingdom; archaeological finds show there was an extensive exchange with Egypt and coastal Syrian cities such as <a href="/wiki/Byblos" title="Byblos">Byblos</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMatthiae2008&#91;httpsarchiveorgdetailsbub_gb_gr5BgOwEJicCpagen60_34&#93;_137-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMatthiae2008[httpsarchiveorgdetailsbub_gb_gr5BgOwEJicCpagen60_34]-137"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>124<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Religion">Religion</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ebla&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Religion"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>Ebla was a <a href="/wiki/Polytheism" title="Polytheism">polytheistic</a> state.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcNultyBrice1996&#91;httpsbooksgooglecombooksidoHMBAwAAQBAJpgPA246_246&#93;_211-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcNultyBrice1996[httpsbooksgooglecombooksidoHMBAwAAQBAJpgPA246_246]-211"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>192<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> During the first kingdom, Eblaites worshiped their dead kings.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStieglitz2002a&#91;httpsbooksgooglecombooksid0Rwals-oh6kCpgPA209_209&#93;_212-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStieglitz2002a[httpsbooksgooglecombooksid0Rwals-oh6kCpgPA209_209]-212"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>193<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The pantheon of the first Ebla included pairs of deities and they can be separated into three genres; in the first and most common one, there were the couples, such as the deity and his female consort.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStieglitz2002a&#91;httpsbooksgooglecombooksid0Rwals-oh6kCpgPA209_209&#93;_212-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStieglitz2002a[httpsbooksgooglecombooksid0Rwals-oh6kCpgPA209_209]-212"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>193<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The second type of pairs was the divine twosomes, such as the deities that cooperate to create the cosmos, like in the Egyptian and Mesopotamian pantheons.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStieglitz2002a&#91;httpsbooksgooglecombooksid0Rwals-oh6kCpgPA209_209&#93;_212-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStieglitz2002a[httpsbooksgooglecombooksid0Rwals-oh6kCpgPA209_209]-212"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>193<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The third type included divine pairs who were actually a single deity that had two names.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStieglitz2002a&#91;httpsbooksgooglecombooksid0Rwals-oh6kCpgPA209_209&#93;_212-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStieglitz2002a[httpsbooksgooglecombooksid0Rwals-oh6kCpgPA209_209]-212"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>193<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Eblaites worshiped few Mesopotamian deities, preferring North-Western Semitic gods, some of which were unique to Ebla.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERendsburg1997&#91;httpoxfordbiblicalstudiescomarticleoprt256e315_hi4_pos56_183&#93;_178-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERendsburg1997[httpoxfordbiblicalstudiescomarticleoprt256e315_hi4_pos56_183]-178"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>162<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The first genre of pairs included <a href="/wiki/Hadabal" title="Hadabal">Hadabal</a> (<sup>d</sup>NI-<i>da</i>-KUL<sup id="cite_ref-213" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-213"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>194<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>), who was exclusive to Ebla, and his consort, Belatu ("his wife");<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStieglitz2002a&#91;httpsbooksgooglecombooksid0Rwals-oh6kCpgPA210_210&#93;_214-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStieglitz2002a[httpsbooksgooglecombooksid0Rwals-oh6kCpgPA210_210]-214"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>195<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Resheph" title="Resheph">Rasap</a> and his consort <a href="/wiki/Adamma_(goddess)" title="Adamma (goddess)">Adamma</a>;<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStieglitz2002a&#91;httpsbooksgooglecombooksid0Rwals-oh6kCpgPA210_210&#93;_214-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStieglitz2002a[httpsbooksgooglecombooksid0Rwals-oh6kCpgPA210_210]-214"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>195<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> the patron gods of the city <a href="/wiki/Kura_(deity)" title="Kura (deity)">Kura</a>, who was unique to Ebla, and his consort <a href="/wiki/Barama_(goddess)" title="Barama (goddess)">Barama</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStieglitz2002a&#91;httpsbooksgooglecombooksid0Rwals-oh6kCpgPA212_212&#93;_215-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStieglitz2002a[httpsbooksgooglecombooksid0Rwals-oh6kCpgPA212_212]-215"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>196<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFrayne2008&#91;httpsbooksgooglecombooksid8PNAnIome3ACpgPT206_41-44&#93;_216-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFrayne2008[httpsbooksgooglecombooksid8PNAnIome3ACpgPT206_41-44]-216"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>197<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The third genre included the artisan god <a href="/wiki/Chemosh" title="Chemosh">Kamish/Tit</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kothar-wa-Khasis" title="Kothar-wa-Khasis">Kothar-wa-Khasis</a> and the planet <a href="/wiki/Venus" title="Venus">Venus</a> represented by twin mountain gods; <a href="/wiki/Shahar_(god)" title="Shahar (god)">Shahar</a> as the morning star and <a href="/wiki/Shalim" title="Shalim">Shalim</a> as the evening star.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStieglitz2002a&#91;httpsbooksgooglecombooksid0Rwals-oh6kCpgPA209_209&#93;_212-4" class="reference"><a
