href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStieglitz2002a[httpsbooksgooglecombooksid0Rwals-oh6kCpgPA209_209]-212"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>193<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>The first Eblaites worshiped many other deities, such as the Syrian goddess <a href="/wiki/I%C5%A1%E1%B8%ABara" title="Išḫara">Ishara</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-220" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-220"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>note 20<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> who was the goddess of the royal family.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEArchi2002&#91;httpsbooksgooglecombooksid5a8-NudlBx8CpgPA28_28&#93;_221-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEArchi2002[httpsbooksgooglecombooksid5a8-NudlBx8CpgPA28_28]-221"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>201<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Ishtar" class="mw-redirect" title="Ishtar">Ishtar</a> was also worshiped but was mentioned only five times in one of the monthly offering lists, while Ishara was far more important, appearing 40 times.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEArchi2002&#91;httpsbooksgooglecombooksid5a8-NudlBx8CpgPA27_27&#93;_222-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEArchi2002[httpsbooksgooglecombooksid5a8-NudlBx8CpgPA27_27]-222"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>202<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Other deities included <a href="/wiki/Damu#The_Eblaite_Damu" title="Damu">Damu</a>;<sup id="cite_ref-224" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-224"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>note 21<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStieglitz1990&#91;httpsbooksgooglecombooksid_MVtAAAAMAAJ_81&#93;_223-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStieglitz1990[httpsbooksgooglecombooksid_MVtAAAAMAAJ_81]-223"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>203<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> the Mesopotamian god <a href="/wiki/Utu" class="mw-redirect" title="Utu">Utu</a>;<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWright2004&#91;httpsbooksgooglecombooksiduvtebmqZZDYCpgPA173_173&#93;_14-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWright2004[httpsbooksgooglecombooksiduvtebmqZZDYCpgPA173_173]-14"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>13<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/A%C5%A1tabi" title="Aštabi">Ashtapi</a>;<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAstour1992&#91;httpsbooksgooglecombooksidrXcvrnUF36EC_10&#93;_225-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAstour1992[httpsbooksgooglecombooksidrXcvrnUF36EC_10]-225"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>204<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Dagon" title="Dagon">Dagan</a>;<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESinger2000&#91;httpsbooksgooglecombooksidgqn7OaEzxIMCpgPA223_223&#93;_226-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESinger2000[httpsbooksgooglecombooksidgqn7OaEzxIMCpgPA223_223]-226"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>205<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Hadad" title="Hadad">Hadad</a> (<i>Hadda</i>) and his consort <a href="/wiki/Halabatu" class="mw-redirect" title="Halabatu">Halabatu</a> ("she of Halab");<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEArchi20104_227-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEArchi20104-227"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>206<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEArchi1994&#91;httpsbooksgooglecombooksidhFRwonqqNCUCpgPA250_250&#93;_228-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEArchi1994[httpsbooksgooglecombooksidhFRwonqqNCUCpgPA250_250]-228"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>207<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Shapash_(Canaanite_goddess)" class="mw-redirect" title="Shapash (Canaanite goddess)">Shipish</a>, the goddess of the sun who had a temple dedicated to her cult.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMatthiae2013b&#91;httpsbooksgooglecombooksid0H0EpSm8aioCpgPA102_102&#93;_229-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMatthiae2013b[httpsbooksgooglecombooksid0H0EpSm8aioCpgPA102_102]-229"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>208<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The four city gates were named after the gods Dagan, Hadda, Rasap and Utu, but it is unknown which gate had which name.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFeliu2003&#91;httpsbooksgooglecombooksidIN9_IRYKKUMCpgPA8_8&#93;_230-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFeliu2003[httpsbooksgooglecombooksidIN9_IRYKKUMCpgPA8_8]-230"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>209<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Overall, the offering list mentioned about 40 deities receiving sacrifices.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWright2004&#91;httpsbooksgooglecombooksiduvtebmqZZDYCpgPA173_173&#93;_14-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWright2004[httpsbooksgooglecombooksiduvtebmqZZDYCpgPA173_173]-14"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>13<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>During the third kingdom, Amorites worshiped common northern Semitic gods; the unique Eblaite deities disappeared.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEArchi2002&#91;httpsbooksgooglecombooksid5a8-NudlBx8CpgPA30_30&#93;_231-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEArchi2002[httpsbooksgooglecombooksid5a8-NudlBx8CpgPA30_30]-231"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>210<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Hadad was the most important god, while Ishtar took Ishara's place and became the city's most important deity apart from Hadad.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEArchi2002&#91;httpsbooksgooglecombooksid5a8-NudlBx8CpgPA31_31&#93;_199-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEArchi2002[httpsbooksgooglecombooksid5a8-NudlBx8CpgPA31_31]-199"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>181<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Biblical_connection_theories">Biblical connection theories</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ebla&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Biblical connection theories"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1236090951"><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Ebla-Biblical_controversy" class="mw-redirect" title="Ebla-Biblical controversy">Ebla-Biblical controversy</a></div>
<p>At the beginning of the process of deciphering the tablets, <a href="/wiki/Giovanni_Pettinato" title="Giovanni Pettinato">Giovanni Pettinato</a> made claims about possible connections between Ebla and the Bible,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChavalas2003&#91;httpsbooksgooglecombooksid60fmNZQzwjYCpgPA41_41&#93;_232-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChavalas2003[httpsbooksgooglecombooksid60fmNZQzwjYCpgPA41_41]-232"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>211<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> citing alleged references in the tablets to the existence of <a href="/wiki/Yahweh" title="Yahweh">Yahweh</a>, <a href="/wiki/Patriarchs_(Bible)" title="Patriarchs (Bible)">the Patriarchs</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sodom_and_Gomorrah" title="Sodom and Gomorrah">Sodom and Gomorrah</a> and other Biblical references.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChavalas2003&#91;httpsbooksgooglecombooksid60fmNZQzwjYCpgPA41_41&#93;_232-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChavalas2003[httpsbooksgooglecombooksid60fmNZQzwjYCpgPA41_41]-232"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>211<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> However, much of the initial media excitement about a supposed Eblaite connections with the Bible, based on preliminary guesses and speculations by Pettinato and others, is now widely discredited and the academic consensus is that Ebla "has no bearing on the Minor Prophets, the historical accuracy of the Biblical Patriarchs, Yahweh worship, or Sodom and Gomorrah".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChavalas2003&#91;httpsbooksgooglecombooksid60fmNZQzwjYCpgPA41_41&#93;_232-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChavalas2003[httpsbooksgooglecombooksid60fmNZQzwjYCpgPA41_41]-232"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>211<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> In Ebla studies, the focus has shifted away from comparisons with the Bible; Ebla is now studied as a civilization in its own right.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChavalas2003&#91;httpsbooksgooglecombooksid60fmNZQzwjYCpgPA41_41&#93;_232-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChavalas2003[httpsbooksgooglecombooksid60fmNZQzwjYCpgPA41_41]-232"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>211<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The claims led to a bitter personal and academic conflict between the scholars involved, as well as what some described as political interference by the Syrian authorities.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcBee_Roberts2002&#91;httpsbooksgooglecombooksid5IXecSZOcS8CpgPA13_13&#93;_233-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcBee_Roberts2002[httpsbooksgooglecombooksid5IXecSZOcS8CpgPA13_13]-233"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>212<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Genetics">Genetics</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ebla&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Genetics"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>Ancient DNA analysis on 10 human remains dating to the Early and Middle <a href="/wiki/Bronze_Age" title="Bronze Age">Bronze Age</a> from Ebla found that Eblaites were a mixture of <a href="/wiki/Chalcolithic" title="Chalcolithic">Copper age</a> Levantines and Mesopotamians, and were genetically similar to contemporaneous Levantines.<sup id="cite_ref-Yilmaz_S_1175_234-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Yilmaz_S_1175-234"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>213<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Haplogroup-wise, one Early Bronze Age (2700-2500 BC) individual carried <a href="/wiki/Haplogroup" title="Haplogroup">haplogroup</a> <a href="/wiki/Haplogroup_E1b1b" class="mw-redirect" title="Haplogroup E1b1b">E1b1b1b2a-M123</a>, a lineage likely linked to the diffusion of <a href="/wiki/Afroasiatic_languages" title="Afroasiatic languages">Afroasiatic languages</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-235" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-235"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>214<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Another Early Bronze Age (2572-2470 cal BCE) individual belonged to <a href="/wiki/Haplogroup_J-M267" title="Haplogroup J-M267">J1a2a1a2-P58</a>, while four Middle Bronze Age (2000-1800 BC) individuals carried haplogroups J1a2a1a2-P58 (x2), <a href="/wiki/Haplogroup_G-M201" title="Haplogroup G-M201">G2a</a> and the <a href="/wiki/West_Asian" class="mw-redirect" title="West Asian">West Asian</a>  <a href="/wiki/Haplogroup_T_(Y-DNA)" class="mw-redirect" title="Haplogroup T (Y-DNA)">T1a1-L162</a> which was present since the middle <a href="/wiki/PPNB" class="mw-redirect" title="PPNB">PPNB</a> <a href="/wiki/Levant" title="Levant">Levant</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Yilmaz_S_1175_234-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Yilmaz_S_1175-234"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>213<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Excavations">Excavations</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ebla&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Excavations"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1236090951"><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Ebla_tablets" title="Ebla tablets">Ebla tablets</a></div>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1237032888/mw-parser-output/.tmulti">.mw-parser-output .tmulti .multiimageinner{display:flex;flex-direction:column}.mw-parser-output .tmulti class="thumbimage"><span typeof="mw:File"><a href="/wiki/File:Calco_di_tavoletta_in_terracotta_da_Ebla_-_Museo_d%27Arte_Orientale_Roma.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img alt="" decoding="async" width="200" height="190" class="mw-file-element" 1.5x, 2x" data-file-width="1483" data-file-height="1407" /></a></span></div><div class="thumbcaption">A tablet from the archive</div></div></div></div></div>
<p>In 1964, Italian archaeologists from the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Rome_La_Sapienza" class="mw-redirect" title="University of Rome La Sapienza">University of Rome La Sapienza</a> under the direction of <a href="/wiki/Paolo_Matthiae" title="Paolo Matthiae">Paolo Matthiae</a> began excavating at Tell Mardikh.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFortin1999&#91;httpsbooksgooglecombooksidiFttAAAAMAAJ_54&#93;_236-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFortin1999[httpsbooksgooglecombooksidiFttAAAAMAAJ_54]-236"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>215<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> In 1968, they recovered a statue dedicated to the goddess Ishtar bearing the name of Ibbit-Lim, mentioning him as king of Ebla.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDeVries2006&#91;httpsbooksgooglecombooksidaOJJAwAAQBAJpgPA67_67&#93;_237-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDeVries2006[httpsbooksgooglecombooksidaOJJAwAAQBAJpgPA67_67]-237"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>216<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> That identified the city, long known from Lagashite and <a href="/wiki/Akkadian_empire" class="mw-redirect" title="Akkadian empire">Akkadian</a> inscriptions.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKipfer2000&#91;httpsbooksgooglecombooksidXneTstDbcC0CpgPA334_334&#93;&#91;httpsbooksgooglecombooksidXneTstDbcC0CpgPA683_683&#93;_238-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKipfer2000[httpsbooksgooglecombooksidXneTstDbcC0CpgPA334_334][httpsbooksgooglecombooksidXneTstDbcC0CpgPA683_683]-238"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>217<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> In the next decade, the team discovered a palace (palace G) dating from <abbr title="circa">c.</abbr><span style="white-space:nowrap;">&#8201;2500–2000</span>&#160;<span class="smallcaps"><span style="font-variant: small-caps; text-transform: lowercase;">BC</span></span>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELiverani2013&#91;httpsbooksgooglecombooksid0d1JAgAAQBAJpgPA129_129&#93;_203-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELiverani2013[httpsbooksgooglecombooksid0d1JAgAAQBAJpgPA129_129]-203"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>185<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Finds in the palaces include a small sculpture made out of precious materials, <a href="/wiki/Lapis_lazuli" title="Lapis lazuli">Lapis lazuli</a>, black stones and <a href="/wiki/Gold" title="Gold">gold</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELiverani2013&#91;httpsbooksgooglecombooksid0d1JAgAAQBAJpgPA129_129&#93;_203-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELiverani2013[httpsbooksgooglecombooksid0d1JAgAAQBAJpgPA129_129]-203"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>185<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Thirteen full and fragmentary lenticular cuneiform tablets were found in the palace throne room, thought to have been there versus the archive because of the city's fall.<sup id="cite_ref-239" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-239"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>218<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> In a storeroom off the throne room, the nearly complete standard of the queen and fragments believed to come from the standard of the king were found.<sup id="cite_ref-240" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-240"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>219<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Other artifacts included wood furniture inlaid with <a href="/wiki/Mother-of-pearl" class="mw-redirect" title="Mother-of-pearl">mother-of-pearl</a> and composite statues created from colored stones.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAkkermansSchwartz2003&#91;httpsbooksgooglecombooksid_4oqvpAHDEoCpgPA271_271&#93;_205-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAkkermansSchwartz2003[httpsbooksgooglecombooksid_4oqvpAHDEoCpgPA271_271]-205"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>187<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> A <a href="/wiki/Silver" title="Silver">silver</a> bowl bearing king Immeya's name was recovered from the "Tomb of the Lord of the Goats", together with Egyptian jewels and an Egyptian ceremonial mace presented by pharaoh Hotepibre.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMatthiae2008&#91;httpsarchiveorgdetailsbub_gb_gr5BgOwEJicCpagen61_35&#93;_120-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMatthiae2008[httpsarchiveorgdetailsbub_gb_gr5BgOwEJicCpagen61_35]-120"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>107<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>About 17,000&#160;<a href="/wiki/Cuneiform_(script)" class="mw-redirect" title="Cuneiform (script)">cuneiform</a> tablet fragments were discovered; when put together, they constitute 2,500&#160;complete tablets, making the archive of Ebla one of the biggest from the <span class="nowrap">3rd millennium <span class="smallcaps"><span style="font-variant: small-caps; text-transform: lowercase;">BC</span></span>.</span><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPitard2001&#91;httpsbooksgooglecombooksid4DVHJRFW3mYCpgPA32_32&#93;_241-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPitard2001[httpsbooksgooglecombooksid4DVHJRFW3mYCpgPA32_32]-241"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>220<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> About 80% of the tablets are written using the usual Sumerian combination of <a href="/wiki/Logogram" title="Logogram">logograms</a> and phonetic signs,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENaveh1982&#91;httpsbooksgooglecombooksidNfFrAAAAIAAJ_28&#93;_242-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENaveh1982[httpsbooksgooglecombooksidNfFrAAAAIAAJ_28]-242"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>221<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> while the others exhibited an innovative, purely phonetic representation using Sumerian cuneiform of a previously unknown Semitic language, which was called "Eblaite".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPettinato1981&#91;httpsarchiveorgdetailsarchivesofeblaan00pett_59&#93;_243-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPettinato1981[httpsarchiveorgdetailsarchivesofeblaan00pett_59]-243"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>222<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Bilingual Sumerian/Eblaite vocabulary lists were found among the tablets, allowing them to be translated.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFeliu2003&#91;httpsbooksgooglecombooksidIN9_IRYKKUMCpgPA8_8&#93;_230-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFeliu2003[httpsbooksgooglecombooksidIN9_IRYKKUMCpgPA8_8]-230"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>209<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The tablets provide many important insights into the cultural, economic and political life in northern Mesopotamia around the middle of the <span class="nowrap">3rd millennium <span class="smallcaps"><span style="font-variant: small-caps; text-transform: lowercase;">BC</span></span>.</span><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEZettler2006&#91;httpsbooksgooglecombooksidcOG-fAFvbXcCpgPA134_134&#93;_244-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEZettler2006[httpsbooksgooglecombooksidcOG-fAFvbXcCpgPA134_134]-244"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>223<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> They also provide insight into the everyday lives of the inhabitants,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFranks2013&#91;httpsbooksgooglecombooksidCNGcAwAAQBAJpgPA2_2&#93;_245-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFranks2013[httpsbooksgooglecombooksidCNGcAwAAQBAJpgPA2_2]-245"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>224<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> and contain information about state revenues, Sumerian-Eblaite dictionaries,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFeliu2003&#91;httpsbooksgooglecombooksidIN9_IRYKKUMCpgPA8_8&#93;_230-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFeliu2003[httpsbooksgooglecombooksidIN9_IRYKKUMCpgPA8_8]-230"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>209<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> diplomatic exchanges with foreign rulers,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBiga200930_246-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBiga200930-246"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>225<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> school texts, hymns and myths.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMichalowski2003&#91;httpsbooksgooglecombooksid8l9X_3rHFdECpgPA453_453&#93;_247-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMichalowski2003[httpsbooksgooglecombooksid8l9X_3rHFdECpgPA453_453]-247"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>226<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Library">Library</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ebla&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Library"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>The over 4000-year-old tablets constitute the oldest library ever found.<sup id="cite_ref-248" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-248"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>227<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWellisch1981_249-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWellisch1981-249"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>228<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> At Ebla, "the archives or library constituted an orderly collection of records at least 500 years older than any other that had been found anywhere before."<sup id="cite_ref-250" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-250"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>229<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> There is evidence of their arrangement and classification.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWellisch1981488–500_251-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWellisch1981488–500-251"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>230<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The larger tablets had originally been stored on shelves, but had fallen onto the floor when the palace was destroyed.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStanley2007&#91;httpsbooksgooglecombooksid3SapTk5iGDkCpgPA141_141&#93;_252-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStanley2007[httpsbooksgooglecombooksid3SapTk5iGDkCpgPA141_141]-252"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>231<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The locations of the fallen tablets allowed the excavators to reconstruct their original positions on the shelves.<sup id="cite_ref-253" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-253"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>232<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> They found the tablets had originally been shelved according to subject.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFranks2013&#91;httpsbooksgooglecombooksidCNGcAwAAQBAJpgPA2_2&#93;_245-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFranks2013[httpsbooksgooglecombooksidCNGcAwAAQBAJpgPA2_2]-245"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>224<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>These features were absent from earlier Sumerian excavations. Sophisticated techniques of arrangement of texts, coupled with their composition, evidence the great antiquity of archival and library practices, which may be far older than was assumed to be the case before the discovery of the Ebla library.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWellisch1981488–500_251-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWellisch1981488–500-251"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>230<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> A sizable portion of the tablets contain literary and lexicographic texts; evidence seems to suggest the collection also served – at least partially – as a true library rather than a collection of archives intended solely for use by the kings, their ministers, and their bureaucracy.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWellisch1981488–500_251-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWellisch1981488–500-251"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>230<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The tablets show evidence of the early transcription of texts into foreign languages and scripts, classification and cataloging for easier retrieval, and arrangement by size, form and content.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWellisch1981488–500_251-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWellisch1981488–500-251"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>230<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The Ebla tablets have thus provided scholars with new insights into the origin of library practices that were in use 4,500&#160;years ago.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWellisch1981488–500_251-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWellisch1981488–500-251"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>230<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>While the absolute chronology of the archive is not yet certain a relative chronology for the 50-year period has been established. Because Ebla did not use Mesopotamian style year names or year numbers and the name of rulers was rarely mentioned in the texts scholars used script changes, grammar changes, and most importantly a <a href="/wiki/Prosopography" title="Prosopography">prosopography</a> of the members of the court, especially the wives and daughters of the king. The most relevant tablets for this effort were a series of yearly metal accounts and monthly linen accounts.<sup id="cite_ref-254" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-254"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>233<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-255" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-255"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>234<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Most of the recovered tablets and tablet fragments were stored at the Idlib Regional Museum in Syria. Their current condition is unknown.
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Legacy">Legacy</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ebla&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Legacy"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>Ebla's first kingdom is an example of early Syrian centralized states,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPorter2012&#91;httpsbooksgooglecombooksidLKQ0fZFTeHkCpgPA199_199&#93;_256-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPorter2012[httpsbooksgooglecombooksidLKQ0fZFTeHkCpgPA199_199]-256"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>235<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> and is considered one of the earliest empires by scholars,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAstour1981&#91;httpsbooksgooglecombooksid1A0OgvXfHlQCpgPA4_4&#93;_43-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAstour1981[httpsbooksgooglecombooksid1A0OgvXfHlQCpgPA4_4]-43"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>40<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELiverani2009&#91;httpsbooksgooglecombooksidbRUMQb_1uKcCpgPA228_228&#93;_257-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELiverani2009[httpsbooksgooglecombooksidbRUMQb_1uKcCpgPA228_228]-257"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>236<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> such as <a href="/wiki/Samuel_Finer" title="Samuel Finer">Samuel Finer</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFiner1997&#91;httpsbooksgooglecombooksidaEziNfjinnMCpgPA172_172&#93;_164-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFiner1997[httpsbooksgooglecombooksidaEziNfjinnMCpgPA172_172]-164"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>149<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Karl_Moore_(academic)" title="Karl Moore (academic)">Karl Moore</a>, who consider it the first-recorded world power.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMooreLewis2009&#91;httpsbooksgooglecombooksidLXWTAgAAQBAJpgPA34_34&#93;_258-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMooreLewis2009[httpsbooksgooglecombooksidLXWTAgAAQBAJpgPA34_34]-258"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>237<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Ebla's discovery changed the former view of Syria's history as a bridge between Mesopotamia and Egypt; it proved the region was a center of civilization in its own right.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcBee_Roberts2002&#91;httpsbooksgooglecombooksid5IXecSZOcS8CpgPA12_12&#93;_259-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcBee_Roberts2002[httpsbooksgooglecombooksid5IXecSZOcS8CpgPA12_12]-259"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>238<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Syrian_Civil_War">Syrian Civil War</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ebla&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: Syrian Civil War"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>As a result of the <a href="/wiki/Syrian_Civil_War" class="mw-redirect" title="Syrian Civil War">Syrian Civil War</a>, excavations of Ebla stopped in March 2011.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMatthiae2013c&#91;httpsbooksgooglecombooksid9jaXngEACAAJ_ix&#93;_260-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMatthiae2013c[httpsbooksgooglecombooksid9jaXngEACAAJ_ix]-260"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>239<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> By 2013, it was under control of an <a href="/wiki/Syrian_opposition" title="Syrian opposition">opposition</a> armed group called Arrows of the Right, who took advantage of its elevated location to use it as an observation point to watch for incoming government air attacks, as well as attempting to protect the site from looting.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChivers2013&#91;httpswwwnytimescom20130407worldmiddleeastsyrian-war-devastates-ancient-siteshtmlpagewantedall_r0_1&#93;_261-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChivers2013[httpswwwnytimescom20130407worldmiddleeastsyrian-war-devastates-ancient-siteshtmlpagewantedall_r0_1]-261"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>240<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-262" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-262"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>241<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Many tunnels were dug and a crypt full of human remains was discovered; the remains were scattered and discarded by the robbers, who hoped to find jewelry and other precious artifacts.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChivers2013&#91;httpswwwnytimescom20130407worldmiddleeastsyrian-war-devastates-ancient-siteshtmlpagewantedall_r0_1&#93;_261-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChivers2013[httpswwwnytimescom20130407worldmiddleeastsyrian-war-devastates-ancient-siteshtmlpagewantedall_r0_1]-261"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>240<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Besides excavations by rebels, nearby villagers also began digging at the site with the aim of finding and looting artifacts; some villagers removed carloads of soil suitable for making <a href="/wiki/Ceramic" title="Ceramic">ceramic</a> liners for <a href="/wiki/Masonry_oven" title="Masonry oven">bread-baking ovens</a> from the tunnels.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChivers2013&#91;httpswwwnytimescom20130407worldmiddleeastsyrian-war-devastates-ancient-siteshtmlpagewantedall_r0_1&#93;_261-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChivers2013[httpswwwnytimescom20130407worldmiddleeastsyrian-war-devastates-ancient-siteshtmlpagewantedall_r0_1]-261"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>240<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-263" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-263"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>242<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>The site was captured by the <a href="/wiki/Syrian_Armed_Forces" title="Syrian Armed Forces">Syrian Armed Forces</a> on 30 January 2020 during the <a href="/wiki/Northwestern_Syria_offensive_(December_2019%E2%80%93March_2020)" class="mw-redirect" title="Northwestern Syria offensive (December 2019–March 2020)">5th Northwestern Syria offensive</a>, along with surrounding villages.<sup id="cite_ref-Los_Angeles_Times_2019_mass_exodus_264-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Los_Angeles_Times_2019_mass_exodus-264"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>243<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-265" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-265"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>244<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-266" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-266"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>245<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="See_also">See also</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ebla&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: See also"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>

<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Notes">Notes</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ebla&amp;action=edit&amp;section=33" title="Edit section: Notes"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1239543626">.mw-parser-output .reflist{margin-bottom:0.5em;list-style-type:decimal}@media screen{.mw-parser-output .reflist{font-size:90%}}.mw-parser-output .reflist .references{font-size:100%;margin-bottom:0;list-style-type:inherit}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns-2{column-width:30em}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns-3{column-width:25em}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns{margin-top:0.3em}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns ol{margin-top:0}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns li{page-break-inside:avoid;break-inside:avoid-column}.mw-parser-output .reflist-upper-alpha{list-style-type:upper-alpha}.mw-parser-output .reflist-upper-roman{list-style-type:upper-roman}.mw-parser-output .reflist-lower-alpha{list-style-type:lower-alpha}.mw-parser-output .reflist-lower-greek{list-style-type:lower-greek}.mw-parser-output .reflist-lower-roman{list-style-type:lower-roman}</style><div class="reflist reflist-lower-alpha">
</div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1239543626"><div class="reflist reflist-columns references-column-width" style="column-width: 40em;">

<li id="cite_note-3"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-3">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">All dates in this article are estimated by the <a href="/wiki/Middle_Chronology" class="mw-redirect" title="Middle Chronology">Middle Chronology</a>, unless stated otherwise.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-24"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-24">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The political weakness started during the short reign of <a href="/w/index.php?title=Adub-Damu&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Adub-Damu (page does not exist)">Adub-Damu</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDolce2008&#91;httpsbooksgooglecombooksidJ9eZToFfVJ0CpgPA68_68&#93;_23-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDolce2008[httpsbooksgooglecombooksidJ9eZToFfVJ0CpgPA68_68]-23"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>22<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-37"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-37">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Probably located along the Euphrates river east of Ebla.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEArchi2011&#91;httpswwwjstororgdiscover105615jcunestud630005_5&#93;_36-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEArchi2011[httpswwwjstororgdiscover105615jcunestud630005_5]-36"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>34<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-63"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-63">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">At first Pettinato supported the Naram-Sin theory before proposing the High dating.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAstour2002&#91;httpsbooksgooglecombooksid0Rwals-oh6kCpgPA63_63&#93;_62-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAstour2002[httpsbooksgooglecombooksid0Rwals-oh6kCpgPA63_63]-62"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>59<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-65"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-65">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="/wiki/Michael_Astour" title="Michael Astour">Michael Astour</a> argues that using the chronology accepted by Pettinato, one obtains the date of 2500&#160;<span class="smallcaps"><span style="font-variant: small-caps; text-transform: lowercase;">BC</span></span> for the reign of <a href="/wiki/Ur-Nanshe" title="Ur-Nanshe">Ur-Nanshe</a> of <a href="/wiki/Lagash" title="Lagash">Lagash</a>, who ruled approximately 150&#160;years prior to Lagash's destruction at the hands of king <a href="/wiki/Lugalzagesi" class="mw-redirect" title="Lugalzagesi">Lugalzagesi</a>. Since Ur-Nanshe ruled in 2500&#160;<span class="smallcaps"><span style="font-variant: small-caps; text-transform: lowercase;">BC</span></span>, and his reign is separated by at least 150&#160;years from Hidar of Mari's reign which saw Ebla's destruction, then the date for that event is pulled beyond 2500&#160;<span class="smallcaps"><span style="font-variant: small-caps; text-transform: lowercase;">BC</span></span> and even 2400&#160;<span class="smallcaps"><span style="font-variant: small-caps; text-transform: lowercase;">BC</span></span>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAstour2002&#91;httpsbooksgooglecombooksid0Rwals-oh6kCpgPA63_63&#93;_62-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAstour2002[httpsbooksgooglecombooksid0Rwals-oh6kCpgPA63_63]-62"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>59<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-67"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-67">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Astour argue that according to the middle chronology used for the 2400&#160;<span class="smallcaps"><span style="font-variant: small-caps; text-transform: lowercase;">BC</span></span> date, Eannatum's reign ended in 2425&#160;<span class="smallcaps"><span style="font-variant: small-caps; text-transform: lowercase;">BC</span></span> and Ebla was not destroyed until 2400&#160;<span class="smallcaps"><span style="font-variant: small-caps; text-transform: lowercase;">BC</span></span>; according to the same chronology Lugalzagesi's reign would have started fifty years after 2400&#160;<span class="smallcaps"><span style="font-variant: small-caps; text-transform: lowercase;">BC</span></span>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAstour2002&#91;httpsbooksgooglecombooksid0Rwals-oh6kCpgPA62_62&#93;_66-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAstour2002[httpsbooksgooglecombooksid0Rwals-oh6kCpgPA62_62]-66"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>61<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-70"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-70">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">At first Matthiae supported the Naram-Sin theory then switched to Sargon.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAstour2002&#91;httpsbooksgooglecombooksid0Rwals-oh6kCpgPA64_64&#93;_69-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAstour2002[httpsbooksgooglecombooksid0Rwals-oh6kCpgPA64_64]-69"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>63<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-76"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-76">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Astour believes that Sargon and his grandson were referring to a city with a similar name in Iraq named "<a href="/wiki/Armani_(kingdom)" title="Armani (kingdom)">Ib-la</a>".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAstour2002&#91;httpsbooksgooglecombooksid0Rwals-oh6kCpgPA65_65&#93;_73-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAstour2002[httpsbooksgooglecombooksid0Rwals-oh6kCpgPA65_65]-73"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>66<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHorowitz1998&#91;httpsbooksgooglecombooksidP8fl8BXpR0MCpgPA82_82&#93;_74-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHorowitz1998[httpsbooksgooglecombooksidP8fl8BXpR0MCpgPA82_82]-74"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>67<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Astour says the archives of Ebla at the time of their destruction correspond to the political situation predating the establishment of the Akkadian empire, not just the reign of Naram-Sin.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAstour2002&#91;httpsbooksgooglecombooksid0Rwals-oh6kCpgPA64_64&#93;_69-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAstour2002[httpsbooksgooglecombooksid0Rwals-oh6kCpgPA64_64]-69"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>63<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> It is also unlikely Sargon was responsible because at the time of their destruction, the Ebla tablets describe <a href="/wiki/Kish_(Sumer)" title="Kish (Sumer)">Kish</a> as independent. Lugalzagesi sacked Kish and was killed by Sargon before Sargon destroyed Ibla or Ebla.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAstour2002&#91;httpsbooksgooglecombooksid0Rwals-oh6kCpgPA72_72&#93;_75-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAstour2002[httpsbooksgooglecombooksid0Rwals-oh6kCpgPA72_72]-75"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>68<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-92"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-92">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Amar-Sin's reign lasted from 2045 to 2037&#160;<span class="smallcaps"><span style="font-variant: small-caps; text-transform: lowercase;">BC</span></span> (middle chronology).<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECrawford2015&#91;httpsbooksgooglecombooksid0nIpBgAAQBAJpgPA83_83&#93;_91-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECrawford2015[httpsbooksgooglecombooksid0nIpBgAAQBAJpgPA83_83]-91"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>83<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-96"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-96">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">"Megum" is thought to have been a title of the ruler of Ebla rather than a personal name.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPinnock2000&#91;httpsbooksgooglecombooksidgKTpAAAAMAAJ_1405&#93;_93-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPinnock2000[httpsbooksgooglecombooksidgKTpAAAAMAAJ_1405]-93"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>84<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> King Ibbit-Lim of the latter third kingdom of Ebla also used this title.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEArchi2002&#91;httpsbooksgooglecombooksid5a8-NudlBx8CpgPA26_26&#93;_94-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEArchi2002[httpsbooksgooglecombooksid5a8-NudlBx8CpgPA26_26]-94"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>85<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> An Eblaite seal that reads the sentence <a href="/w/index.php?title=Ib-Damu&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Ib-Damu (page does not exist)">Ib-Damu</a> Mekim Ebla, was used in the <span class="nowrap">19th century <span class="smallcaps"><span style="font-variant: small-caps; text-transform: lowercase;">BC</span></span></span> by an Assyrian merchant named Assur-Nada from <a href="/wiki/K%C3%BCltepe" title="Kültepe">Kültepe</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEArchi2002&#91;httpsbooksgooglecombooksid5a8-NudlBx8CpgPA25_25&#93;_95-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEArchi2002[httpsbooksgooglecombooksid5a8-NudlBx8CpgPA25_25]-95"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>86<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Ib Damu was the name of an Eblaite king from the early period of the first kingdom.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEArchi2002&#91;httpsbooksgooglecombooksid5a8-NudlBx8CpgPA25_25&#93;_95-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEArchi2002[httpsbooksgooglecombooksid5a8-NudlBx8CpgPA25_25]-95"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>86<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-99"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-99">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">In a tablet, the name of Ili-Dagan "the man of Ebla" is mentioned, and he was thought to be a ruler.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGoetze1953&#91;httpswwwjstororgstable1359547_103&#93;_97-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGoetze1953[httpswwwjstororgstable1359547_103]-97"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>87<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> However, other texts mentions him as the envoy of Ebla's ruler.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMichalowski1995&#91;httpsbooksgooglecombooksidthIxCmwfNoMCpgPA185_185&#93;_98-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMichalowski1995[httpsbooksgooglecombooksidthIxCmwfNoMCpgPA185_185]-98"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>88<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-105"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-105">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Unidentified location to the north of Ebla in the proximity of <a href="/wiki/Alalakh" title="Alalakh">Alalakh</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEArchi2002&#91;httpsbooksgooglecombooksid5a8-NudlBx8CpgPA24_24&#93;_104-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEArchi2002[httpsbooksgooglecombooksid5a8-NudlBx8CpgPA24_24]-104"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>93<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-116"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-116">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">This led Astour, David I. Owen and Ron Veenker to identify Ibbit-Lim with the pre-Amorite Megum of the Third Ur era.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKlengel1992&#91;httpsbooksgooglecombooksid5GptAAAAMAAJ_41&#93;_115-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKlengel1992[httpsbooksgooglecombooksid5GptAAAAMAAJ_41]-115"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>103<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> However, this identification is now refuted.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEArchi2002&#91;httpsbooksgooglecombooksid5a8-NudlBx8CpgPA26_26&#93;_94-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEArchi2002[httpsbooksgooglecombooksid5a8-NudlBx8CpgPA26_26]-94"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>85<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-143"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-143">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Called the southern palace (in area&#160;"FF"), it was located at the foot of the southern side of the acropolis.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBonacossi2013&#91;httpsbooksgooglecombooksid5H4fAgAAQBAJpgPT446_422&#93;_142-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBonacossi2013[httpsbooksgooglecombooksid5H4fAgAAQBAJpgPT446_422]-142"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>129<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-146"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-146">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Area HH.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBonacossi2013&#91;httpsbooksgooglecombooksid5H4fAgAAQBAJpgPT446_422&#93;_142-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBonacossi2013[httpsbooksgooglecombooksid5H4fAgAAQBAJpgPT446_422]-142"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>129<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-174"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-174">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The ritual had the king and the queen visiting <a href="/wiki/Binnish" title="Binnish">Ninas</a>, and making offerings to royal ancestors.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBiga2007&#91;httpswwwacademiaedu12271170Buried_among_the_Living_at_Ebla_256&#93;_173-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBiga2007[httpswwwacademiaedu12271170Buried_among_the_Living_at_Ebla_256]-173"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>158<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-181"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-181">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Grammatically, Eblaite is closer to Akkadian, but lexically and in some grammatical forms, Eblaite is closer to West Semitic languages.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHooks1990&#91;httpsbooksgooglecombooksidgoq0VWw9rGICpgPA226_226&#93;_113-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHooks1990[httpsbooksgooglecombooksidgoq0VWw9rGICpgPA226_226]-113"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>101<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-190"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-190">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The Kunga is a hybrid of a <a href="/wiki/Donkey" title="Donkey">donkey</a> and a female <a href="/wiki/Onager" title="Onager">onager</a>, which Nagar was famous for breeding.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKuz&#39;mina2007&#91;httpsbooksgooglecombooksidx5J9rn8p2-ICpgPA134_134&#93;_189-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKuz&#39;mina2007[httpsbooksgooglecombooksidx5J9rn8p2-ICpgPA134_134]-189"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>172<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-210"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-210">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Archaeologist Alessandro de Maigret deduced that Ebla retained its trading position.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAstour2002&#91;httpsbooksgooglecombooksid0Rwals-oh6kCpgPA78_78&#93;_83-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAstour2002[httpsbooksgooglecombooksid0Rwals-oh6kCpgPA78_78]-83"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>75<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-220"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-220">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">At the beginning of Ebla's studies, it was believed that the existence of Ishara and another god <i><a href="/wiki/A%C5%A1tabi" title="Aštabi">Ashtapi</a></i> in Ebla's pantheon, is a proof for a Hurrian existence in the Eblaite first kingdom.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBiga2014&#91;httpsbooksgooglecombooksidAcnmBQAAQBAJpgPA100_100&#93;_217-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBiga2014[httpsbooksgooglecombooksidAcnmBQAAQBAJpgPA100_100]-217"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>198<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> However it is now known that those deities were pre-Hurrian and perhaps pre-Semitic deities, later incorporated into the Hurrian pantheon.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEArchi2002&#91;httpsbooksgooglecombooksid5a8-NudlBx8CpgPA31_31&#93;_199-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEArchi2002[httpsbooksgooglecombooksid5a8-NudlBx8CpgPA31_31]-199"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>181<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWestenholz1999&#91;httpsbooksgooglecombooksidJgA-ez-BLbECpgPA155_155&#93;_218-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWestenholz1999[httpsbooksgooglecombooksidJgA-ez-BLbECpgPA155_155]-218"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>199<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFleming2000&#91;httpsbooksgooglecombooksidmN6pOFeouXICpgPA208_208&#93;_219-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFleming2000[httpsbooksgooglecombooksidmN6pOFeouXICpgPA208_208]-219"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>200<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-224"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-224">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Probably an old Semitic deity and not identical to the Sumerian <a href="/wiki/Damu" title="Damu">Damu</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStieglitz1990&#91;httpsbooksgooglecombooksid_MVtAAAAMAAJ_81&#93;_223-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStieglitz1990[httpsbooksgooglecombooksid_MVtAAAAMAAJ_81]-223"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>203<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup></span>
</li>
</ol></div>
